## 771

B"H, 11 Nissan, 5713
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, etc., occupied with communal needs,
Rabbi Nachum, may he live and be well,

Shalom u’Bracha!

In response to your letter—which lacks the date of writing—in which you ask my opinion regarding your moving to the United States.

It is surprising that this is even a question for you. For my opinion is well-known—I do not understand, nor do I support, the rush to relocate, and in the vast majority of cases, I do not support the relocation itself. This is especially true regarding a person who has merited that Divine Providence has chosen him to bring the hearts of Bnei Yisrael closer to their Father in Heaven through the "light" of Torah—namely, the teachings of Chassidus, its ways, and its practices. Furthermore, [Providence] has provided him with a situation where his material livelihood is combined with his spiritual livelihood. Consequently, it is clear to me that this is the channel for him and for all his family members, may they live, through which they will receive everything they need, both materially and spiritually. This is all contingent upon his standing firm at his post to fulfill his role as required, to the extent that it depends on him—and even this is sufficient, for "according to the camel is the load."

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach.

272

B"H, 11 Nissan, 5713
Brooklyn.

Mr. Azriel, may he live.

It is with pleasure that I receive news from the distinguished Chossid, G-d-fearing and upright, involved in communal needs, one of the elder Rabbonim of Anash, Rabbi Yisroel Noach Belinitzky, may he live, regarding your attitude toward him, your care for his affairs, and above all, your attention to everything that Rabbi Yisroel Noach, may he live, requires.

And if, in a general sense, our Sages of blessed memory expounded on the verse "and he shall cause him to be thoroughly healed" (Shemos 21:19) that this teaches that the Torah grants permission to a physician to heal, yet there was a concern in this matter—that perhaps people would rely on the physician himself and forget the G-d who made them, which is the reason why, according to many commentators, Chizkiyahu hid the Book of Remedies—then when the physician himself knows that he is the messenger of the Healer of all flesh, who performs wonders, our G-d, then naturally there is no room for this concern, and his medical treatments are most successful. May the Ribono shel Olam send blessing and success in your endeavors, that you may be the proper messenger to bring healing to the ailments of the people whom Divine Providence brings to your hand. And the reward of a mitzvah is a mitzvah, that also in your own personal affairs, blessing and success shall be drawn down for everything you, your wife, and your children, may they live, require, both materially and spiritually.

With holiday blessings.

## 273

B"H, 11 Nissan, 5713
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi and Chassid, G-d-fearing and distinguished, occupied with communal needs,
Rabbi Ephraim Eliezer, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter in which you commented on the precise and wondrous language of Rashi (Ki Sisa 33:12), which states: "See, set your eyes and your heart upon your words." I will also contribute my share regarding Rashi’s intent, and also **from where Rashi derives this.**

The ultimate purpose of Moshe’s aforementioned request was that even though Yisroel had sinned, the *hashraas haShechinah* should nevertheless remain among them—His very Essence and Being, *yisbarach*, and not through an intermediary, nor even through an angel (see *Likutei Torah*, Tzav 12c). This is as he concludes in the verse, "If Your Presence does not go... and we will be distinguished, I and Your people..." which is the concept of a *dirah lo yisbarach batachtonim*—"Make Me a sanctuary and I will dwell among them," within each and every one through the *Mikdash*. Consequently, we know Moshe’s intent from Hashem’s promise regarding the *Beis HaMikdash*, as it is stated: "And My eyes and My heart shall be there **all the days**"—meaning, even during a time of wrath.

And with this, we can understand the puzzling *Yerushalmi*—*Berachos*, Chapter 4, Halacha 5—"His face is above, and His eyes and His heart are below," see there. Based on this, we can also understand the precision of Rashi’s language, as well as why he concludes "upon your words," which refers to the *sefirah* of *Malchus*, through which the world was created, whose ultimate purpose and inwardness is the *Beis HaMikdash*. For the entirety of the world is a *dirah lo yisbarach*, and within the world itself, this is the concept of the *Beis HaMikdash*. Based on this, one can also explain the matter of "His eyes and His heart"—in accordance with what is explained in *Tikkunei Zohar*, *Tikkun* 21, page 50b, and *Raza D'Mila*, etc.

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach, and awaiting good news regarding the matters we discussed here, and the sooner the better.

P.S. Enclosed is a copy of a general letter which I am sure will interest you.

## 276

B.H., 11 Nissan, 5713
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 17 Adar, in which you raise a question regarding the wording of an invitation you received from the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, and learned individual, R. Ben Zion Shem Tov, who is involved in shlichus, etc. In the invitation, he expresses his blessing that his son should be a G-d-fearing person, a Chossid, and a scholar, "according to my intention and desire." You found it difficult to understand how one can link the expression of a Jew’s heartfelt wishes to the intention and desire of a mortal.

It is well known that tefillah is an avodah **of the heart**, as our Sages have said (Taanis 2a), and it must be expressed **in speech**. Consequently, one’s mouth and heart must be in accord for the tefillah to be effective. This is also understood from the text of our tefillos: the more a person specifies their prayer, the more praiseworthy it is, rather than settling for a general prayer. This is why we pray the twelve middle blessings of the Amidah, rather than a single, all-encompassing request. Furthermore, within these very blessings, there are many details specified in each one. Therefore, one who does not trust in their own ability to specify their prayer as required—for whatever reason—and who believes that one of their friends, who truly wishes them well, has a more detailed and profound understanding of what they need, will formulate their prayer and request such that it be fulfilled according to the intention and desire of that specific individual. We find similar concepts in Halacha in many areas, such as the *heter iska*, where it is written that it is done "according to the enactment of our Sages." Even an unlearned person writes this, and it is effective according to the law of the Torah, because they defer their own intent to that of our Sages.

2) Furthermore, who knows the inclination of the human heart and the stratagems of the *yetzer hara*? He is a master craftsman who approaches each person according to their nature, as our Sages have said (Shabbos 105b). Consequently, he seeks various ways to confuse a person, especially at a time of *ratzon* (divine favor), when one fears that their prayer might be accepted. There is greater room for concern that he will confuse the person and introduce some ulterior motive into their request and prayer. The more the request is elevated and distanced from material matters—as in the aforementioned request to be G-d-fearing—and he is not satisfied with that, but adds that he should also be a *Chossid* (meaning, going beyond the letter of the law, even of G-d-fearingness), and is not satisfied with that, but adds further that he should be a *lamdan* (meaning, that he should utilize the power of Torah to be G-d-fearing and a Chossid, for the Torah is the Torah of truth, and "Chesed is the foundation of the world")—it is understood that the *yetzer hara* will seek every means at his disposal to confuse the prayer. If he cannot do so in speech, he will do so in the heart. This is similar to what the Rambam writes at the end of *Hilchos Temurah*: "The Torah has plumbed the depths of human thought and the nature of the evil inclination..." Even though one has made a vow or a dedication, it is possible that they have had second thoughts and regretted it. The only advice for this is to entrust one’s prayer to the intention of another person who is not personally involved and who seeks one’s benefit, for then the speech and the intention will proceed in unison. There are many examples of this in the *nigleh* (revealed Torah), one of which is from our Sages (Nedarim 25a): "He said to them, 'Know that I am not placing you under oath according to your intention, but according to my intention and the intention of the Omnipresent.'"

3) You also ask that, seemingly, it is simple that prayer should generally be to be among those who do the will of Hashem, without specifying the path one desires. With all due respect, this is not so. As mentioned above, from all the texts of the prayers established by the Men of the Great Assembly, it is evident that they specified requests and prayers as much as possible. The reason they did not specify even more is so that there would be one uniform text for all the children of Israel. However, even this text is highly detailed, as mentioned above (see Rambam, *Hilchos Tefillah*, chapters 1 and 6).

I will tell you the truth: I was in doubt whether to answer these questions or not. Unfortunately, in all times, and especially in our time, there are many whose intent is the questioning itself (especially regarding a path to which they are not accustomed; when they ask themselves why they are shying away from it, the easiest excuse is to say that they have "questions" about the path). In such a case, there is not much benefit in the answer, since the gates of questioning are never locked. However, since I do not know you, I presume you to be righteous, and that your intent is surely only for the good—that is, to explain to yourself a matter that you have not yet encountered in the correspondence of others, even though examples of it are found everywhere, as mentioned above.

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach.

P.S. Of course, if you have any comments on the above, I would be interested in reading them, and I will endeavor to answer them to the best of my ability.

**Note:** I specifically brought the two examples above because the matter of an oath is a matter of human *avodah* that begins from the time of Bar Mitzvah—when one becomes "sworn and standing" from Mount Sinai. And the intent regarding *ribis* (interest) in a *heter iska* is as explained in the *maamar* of the Rebbe Rayatz, *d.h. Ta'ama Ki Tov Sachrah*, from the year 5709.

Enclosed is a copy of my general letter for the upcoming Pesach, which I am sure will interest you.

## 277

B"H, 11 Nissan, 5713
Brooklyn

To the venerable Rabbi, the esteemed Chassid, who is involved in the work of Heaven, Rabbi Yitzchak, may he live:

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter—for which my reply was delayed due to various reasons, and for which I ask your forgiveness.

Regarding your inquiry, continuing from our previous correspondence, where your question was that since the language of the *Biurei HaZohar* implies that the *yichud* of Yaakov and Rachel has ceased, this would also preclude the concept of *hashpa'ah* (influence) through the *sefirah* of *Malchus*, even as a mere conduit.

To my mind, however, the laws of *yichud* prove the contrary. The point regarding this is explained in a general and well-known introduction: that the existence of the *sefiros* and a portion of their activity exist even without the *avodah* of man. This is brought in many places in Kabbalah and *Chassidus* regarding the distinction between the external *yichud* of *Chochmah* and *Binah* and the internal *yichud* of *Chochmah* and *Binah*. The external *yichud* does not depend on the *avodah* of man; regarding this it is said, "They shall not cease," etc. Even after the Giving of the Torah, there are several such matters. Furthermore, this applies even within the Torah itself, similar to the concept that "Shabbos is sanctified and established [by Hashem]," whereas with *Yom Tov*, "Israel sanctifies the times." See the introduction to *Pri Etz Chaim*, which states that within the *sefiros* themselves, both these aspects exist, as mentioned above.

Likewise, in the *avodah* of man himself, when he draws [the light] down to the letters of thought, and certainly in speech, and even more so in action, this causes an *hashpa'ah* to occur above in the *sefirah* of *Malchus*. It is understood that this is more potent in action than in speech, and in speech more than in thought—and certainly when the speech reaches another person below, and even more so when there is a *yichud* of *ZON* (*Ze'er Anpin* and *Nukvah*), through which a completely new entity is born. An example of this is also found in the various prophecies where the prophets performed a physical act, through which the prophecy was drawn down to a much lower level, making it difficult to nullify the prophecy, as explained in the *sefarim*. See also the letter of our Rebbe HaZaken in the *Biur* (in the *Siddur Meah Shearim*) regarding how the Maggid, when a matter would occur to him in thought, would also draw it down into speech.

Based on the above, it is understood that even though with the *histalkus* of Rachel the *yichud* of Yaakov and Rachel has ceased, this is not a contradiction at all to *hashpa'ah* to the *sefirah* of *Malchus* through a conduit, and furthermore—even in the aspect of *hislabshus* (this comes as an addition to the first letter). However, the birth of a completely new entity—one in which both extremes are found, the birth of something similar to itself and an essence in its own right—this is impossible. This is similar to what is brought in many places regarding the superiority of the influence of the "drop" (the essence) over the influence of the intellect, even though, naturally, the influence of the intellect also passes through the *sefirah* of *Malchus*, meaning it undergoes several *tzimtzumim* and *hislabshus*, etc.

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach,

Regarding what you ask about my *maamarim* and *sichos*: for the time being, apart from a few individual ones, I have not yet organized them. However, I am enclosing a copy of my general letter for the upcoming Pesach, which I hope will be of interest to you.

Surely the matter discussed regarding the editing of the *seder* in public is still in effect this year as well.

## 278

B"H, 13 Nissan, 5713
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and distinguished individual, etc.,
Rabbi Tzvi, may he live,

Greetings and blessings!

It was with pleasure that I received your letter of 1 Adar, in which you write regarding your settlement and business in the Holy Land, may it be rebuilt and established. Surely—even though you do not mention it in your letter—you participate from time to time in a Chassidic farbrengen. This is a matter for which there is no substitute in any other activity, as is explained in several of the sichos of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, which you have surely seen.

I conveyed your regards to my mother, the Rabbanit, may she live, and she was pleased to hear of your circumstances, as she remembers your entire family well and even recounted to me several events concerning you personally.

I see that you are involved in publishing, and surely you are utilizing this opportunity to increase the light of Torah and Yiddishkeit in the Holy Land, may it be rebuilt and established. As is well known, the Tzemach Tzedek’s adage is that a word that goes to print is for generations to come; consequently, the merit and responsibility resting upon the publisher are understood.

As you requested, when I am at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, I will mention you and your family, may they live, for whatever you may need.

Approaching the days of the festival that are coming upon us and upon all of Israel for good, I hereby express my blessing for a kosher and happy Pesach.

And for the fulfillment of the hoped-for promise, "As in the days of your exodus from the land of Egypt, I will show you wonders," in our true and complete redemption, in his days and in our days, through Moshiach Tzidkeinu.

With festival blessings.

P.S. In response to your question: I am interested in all printed materials that have any connection to Jews and Yiddishkeit. Many have already adopted the custom of sending to my library everything printed by them or published under their auspices. Although I do not have a fund for such expenses, my library exchanges these for books published by Kehot for those who desire this. You have surely seen the list of Kehot publications, as there are several catalogs among Anash in the Holy Land, may it be rebuilt and established.

## 279

B.H., 13 Nissan, 5713
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the esteemed Rabbi, man of action, engaged in communal needs, a man of energy and many accomplishments, etc., etc., Rabbi Zev, may he live.

Greetings and Blessings,

I received your letter in due time, as well as your gift, your book "Nivei Zahav." Many thanks for your thoughtfulness.

From time to time, I read about your travels, and I also receive reports about you from the countries you have visited. May it be Hashem’s will that you utilize all these opportunities to increase and glorify the Torah of Hashem.

In connection with the above and the content of your letter regarding the various statements of our Sages concerning Rav, and the words of the wise which are gracious, I have come to fulfill your request (it is surprising that you did not specifically mention the statement of our Sages regarding Rav that follows, which is the most characteristic line in the life and activities of Rav, and from which there is a clear and fundamental instruction for our current days). This is the statement of our Sages (Eruvin 6a): "Rav found a valley and built a fence around it." This is the sign: When Rav came from **Eretz Yisrael** (and the interpretation of our Sages in Bereishis Rabbah is well known: why is it called *Eretz*? Because it desired [*ratzah*] to do the will of its Creator; and *Yisrael*, as it is said, "For you have struggled [*sarita*] with Elokim and men"—even in the darkness of the night, and even when left alone—and you have prevailed) to Bavel (which was "mixed" [*belulah*], where light and darkness serve in confusion, a mixture of good and evil, holy and profane, a blurring of boundaries), he distanced himself in Bavel to a place where there was no Torah (Iggeres Rav Sherira Gaon). In the words of Rashi: "He found a valley," meaning they were negligent in the observance of mitzvos. (Seemingly, this is puzzling: how is it possible for a Jewish person to be negligent in the mitzvos of his Father in Heaven, which are his very life—and one who separates from them is like a fish separating from the sea? As is well known from the saying of our Rebbe, the Alter Rebbe: "A Yid neither wants to, nor can he be, torn away from Elokus." Therefore, Rashi explains the reason for this negligence: they were *amei ha'aretz*—ignorant of Torah.)

In such a state, winds blow in the world, and people teach that one should lighten the yoke of Torah and mitzvos and make compromises—at least for a time—in order to draw the hearts of the *amei ha'aretz* and bring them closer to Torah and mitzvos by lightening the burden upon them. They remove from their bodies and their animal souls the yoke of the Kingdom of Heaven, and they dress this in the guise of "fear of Heaven," arguing that it is worth sacrificing the secondary to preserve the primary, etc.

However, Rav—the teacher of the children of the Diaspora, who brought the Torah of our holy Rebbe to Bavel—drove stakes into the ground: If there is a valley, that is not the way to make compromises to lighten the load. As the prophet says, "Those who draw iniquity with cords of vanity, and sin as with a cart rope." As is known from the parable we heard from my father-in-law, the Rebbe: A man walking on the king’s highway who, over time, finds himself in the darkness of the forest among wild beasts—this did not happen all at once, that he jumped from the road into the thickets of the forest. Rather, the initial deviation was as thin as a hair, and hair by hair it accumulated into a large area.

The instruction of Rav for his days and for all generations, and especially in our time—a time of blurring boundaries and confusion of values like no other—is that where there is a "valley," one must, on the contrary, build a fence and add an additional safeguard. Only in this way is the valley preserved, and the people within it preserved.

I see in your book that Hashem has graced you with a "learned tongue," and I have heard as much from several people who were present at your speeches. May it be the will of the Almighty that you use this talent to walk on the paved path mentioned above, which has been handed down to us by our Sages in the Diaspora (Sanhedrin 17b): that wherever one finds a valley—whether in the Diaspora, and all the more so in the Land upon which the eyes of Hashem your G-d are always fixed, the palace of the King of Kings, the Holy One, blessed be He—one should build a fence around it. And the promise of the Almighty is well known: "Open for Me an opening the size of a needle's eye, and I will open for you an opening as wide as the entrance to the Hall."

It is characteristic that in the three places cited in the Shas where it is said that Rav "found a valley and built a fence," the subject is the establishment of a partition and the prevention of the blurring of boundaries—whether in the inanimate, the vegetative, or the living: regarding a partition and distinction in the inanimate, in space—between the private domain (*reshus hayachid*—the domain of the Unique One of the world) and the public domain (*reshus harabim*) (Eruvin 6a); the distinction between (the human) "tree of the field," which is moist and alive (through "and you who cleave to Hashem your G-d—are all alive") and that which is dry and dead (Eruvin 100b); and the fence regarding the law of meat and milk (Chullin 110a)—the essence of which is the prohibition of *kilayim* (forbidden mixtures) in living things and the mixture of species that are not of their own kind (Tikkunei Zohar, Tikkun 14; Recanati and Shelah, Parshas Mishpatim, and elsewhere).

With respect and with blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach.

Enclosed is a copy of a general letter that will surely interest you.

## B'P

B'P

B"H, 13 Nissan, 5713
Brooklyn.

This applies generally to all matters involving danger during wartime. You should obtain the sefer *Machaneh Yisrael* by the Chofetz Chaim; it addresses the various questions relevant to soldiers. May Hashem Yisbarach protect you wherever you may be, and may you return home whole and healthy in its proper time.

## 284

B”H, 13 Nissan, 5713
Brooklyn.

I received your letter from 4 Nissan, along with the *pidyon nefesh* enclosed within it, which I will, G-d willing, read at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory.

It goes without saying that you are careful with *tevilas Ezra*. May Hashem grant you success in your Torah study with *yiras shamayim*, without any imagined disturbances or obstacles, which come only to increase the reward and the preciousness of the matter.

With blessings for a kosher and happy *Pesach*, and for success in Torah study with *yiras shamayim*.

After all is said and done, it is possible that all of this may remain only as a *makif* (encompassing influence) upon them, and it depends entirely on one’s efforts to ensure that the matter is internalized (*pnimiyus*) for their benefit—not only in spiritual matters, but in material ones as well. Given the nature of the bochurim, you should explain this to them, whether by speaking with each one individually—where you can speak *na'henter* (more closely/personally)—or, conversely, with everyone together, where it is possible to speak *sharper* (more sharply/incisively), since you are not singling out any one person but addressing the many.

## 288

B"H, 24 Nissan, 5713
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

I received your inquiry—albeit with some delay—the content of which is as follows: Having found manuscripts of Chassidic maamarim and seeing the title "Kisvei Kodesh" (Holy Writings) inscribed on the cover, you ask whether it is permissible to refer to other books by the name "Kisvei Kodesh," given that this term is also used for the books of Tanach.

It is surprising that you are unaware that many books have been titled with names such as these, for example: *Mishneh Torah* by the Rambam, *Toras Moshe* by Rabbi Moshe Alshich, and *Shnei Luchos HaBris* by the Shelah (I mention only these few, and you will find many more in bibliographical lists). "By the testimony of two or three witnesses shall a matter be established." May it be Hashem's will that, now that you see your difficulty is no difficulty at all, you will at least agree to establish a constant shiur in the Kisvei Kodesh of our Rebbeim, may their merit protect us.

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
The Secretary

## 289

B"H, 27 Nissan, 5713
Brooklyn

To the esteemed Rabbi and Chossid, G-d-fearing and meticulous, engaged in communal affairs,
Rabbi Yechiel Michel, may he be well.

Greetings and blessings!

Before the holiday of Pesach, your letter was received—though it lacked the date of writing. I was pleased to read that matters have been settled in the shul of the late R' M.M. Kupershtoch, and that you have secured all matters pertaining to the esteemed Rabbi Chen, may he be well. May it be the will of Hashem that you succeed in the shul and its surroundings in general, to glorify and disseminate there the "lamp of mitzvah and the light of Torah," and the light of Torah—this being the Torah of Chassidus, its guidance, and its conduct.

2) Regarding your desire to distinguish in the matter of the second day of Yom Tov in the Diaspora between those who move to Eretz Yisrael and those who do otherwise: From the discussions in the Gemara—specifically in Pesachim 51a and Megillah 19a—it is understood that one should not make such a distinction in matters pertaining to halacha. Even regarding that which depends on the stringencies of local custom, which is discussed in Pesachim regarding "who follows whom," this is not applicable in the case at hand, as is understood. As for your inquiry and precision regarding the language of the Alter Rebbe in his Shulchan Aruch, at the end of Siman 496, concerning the difference between paragraph 7 and paragraph 11: it must be that in paragraph 7 he relies on what is stated in paragraph 11, which proves from its beginning that the matter depends on a dispute. This is consistent with the Alter Rebbe’s approach in many places in his Shulchan Aruch: to transcribe the laws in the manner and reality in which he found them in the books and authorities that preceded him, similar to the general rule of the Rambam in such matters.

3) Regarding your note on the word *l'zman* (for a time), where I wrote that this is how it appears in the Siddur Shelah, and that in your printing you found it punctuated with a *patach*: My intention was to the first edition. For afterwards, it was dependent on the printers, who changed several matters in the prayers themselves, and it was called "Siddur Shelah" only after the commentary—especially in a matter where they did not know that there was a precise nuance here.

4) Regarding what you wrote as to whether one should answer "Amen" in Bircas Hamazon after "Al Yechasreinu": I once asked the Rebbe Rayatz, and he replied that he does not answer. Furthermore, one of the close associates of the Rebbe Rashab would answer "Amen" after "Al Yechasreinu" in his presence in a loud voice, and the Rebbe smiled and said nothing. Perhaps they rely on the end of the blessing, where it says "v'neimar Amen," though even in this I have not seen the listeners take care to answer "Amen" at that point. This is an opportunity for me to note in general that it is strange that in several prayers we conclude with "v'neimar Amen," yet the listeners do not do so, and this requires study.

5) Regarding the blessing of "Shepatrani me'onesh halazeh," whether to recite it with Hashem’s name and kingship: It seems that it has already been printed* to recite it without Hashem’s name and kingship, and therefore it is printed this way in the Tehillat Hashem Siddur (Rostov), which is accurate. As for the fact that the Nesi'im recited it with Hashem’s name and kingship, as brought in a sicha of the Rebbe Rayatz, this is not an instruction for the public. This is what I heard from the Rebbe Rayatz.

With blessings for success in your holy work, in good health.

## 209

27 Nissan, 5713
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the Administration of the Kupas Bochurim of Yeshivas Tomchei Temimim Lubavitch, may Hashem grant them life.

Peace and blessing!

I was pleased to receive your letter containing a description of your work and a report. May it be the will of Hashem that you succeed in your work, both materially and spiritually.

What is surprising is that an institution connected to my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, should restrict its expenditures so strictly that they do not exceed its income; I believe this is the only institution within the four cubits of Lubavitch that conducts itself in this manner. I would not intervene in this matter were it not for my concern that, because of this policy of not allowing expenses to exceed income, you are also restricting your work in areas where it is necessary. It is certainly superfluous to arouse you regarding the attribute of *bitachon* in general; I am only offering this note and insight: it is worthwhile to utilize the attribute of *bitachon* in this specific matter as well. You should see that if you increase your expenditures accordingly, larger sources of income will certainly be provided to you. It is possible that, on the contrary, this is the proper path, as explained in Chassidus regarding the distinction between limbs and vessels—that limbs do not merely receive vitality, but also draw down vitality. From this, it is understood that when the channels of the limbs are expanded, they draw down greater vitality.

In accordance with all the above, so that you will have the means to utilize them in a place or instance of a deficit, I am enclosing a *gemach* loan from one of the funds in my possession in the amount of two hundred shekels. Consequently, you have the ability to increase your expenditures immediately by at least two hundred shekels beyond your income.

With a blessing to all those involved in this fund, those who participate and assist them, that Hashem may grant the House of Chabad the merit that they will only be compelled to provide *gemach* loans not out of material necessity, but only in order to thereby become a *merkavah* for the *sefirah* of *Chesed* of *Atzilus*, as recounted in the *sicha* of my father-in-law, the Rebbe (5701).

With blessings for success and all the best.

## 292

[Month of Geulah, 5713]

Regarding the difficulty you raised in your last letter concerning the conclusion in Niddah (42a) that it is considered as if she saw [a discharge], based on the Tosefta in Zavim (1:3), and your argument that according to the Gra’s commentary, if it is not of its own kind it does not invalidate [the clean days], and thus it should be considered as if she merely touched [a source of impurity]—I did not understand your question. The Gemara is discussing one who emits semen that she received, while the Tosefta is discussing one who sees a discharge that is not of its own kind, where even the impurity of touching does not result from this, because a woman does not become impure through white discharge [in that manner].

2) Regarding the difficulty you raised there in your letter—concerning the Rash in Zavim (4:6), who explains the aforementioned Tosefta that "its own kind does not invalidate it" means a Zav who saw [a discharge] etc., which only invalidates one day, whereas a Zavah invalidates seven days; if so, this is a stringency for a Zavah that does not apply to a Zav.

Indeed, even **regarding the law itself**, one could raise a difficulty: since the scriptural decree is that only a complete Zivah invalidates all seven days, and not when it is mixed, then this law should also apply to a Zavah. Thus, if she sees white discharge during her days of Zivah, even though it is impossible without traces of Zivah, it should only invalidate one day (see Niddah 22a and Tosafos there).

Furthermore, according to this interpretation, it is still difficult—he should have said "its mixed kind does not invalidate."

Also, on the face of it, it is difficult to understand what the Rash meant when he wrote that regarding the second interpretation there are several difficulties—what are they?

To resolve all the above observations, and since in any case it is necessary to say that the Rash on this Mishnah was printed incorrectly, as between the words "a woman in seeing" **several** words are missing, as is obvious: perhaps it can be suggested that another error occurred in the printing, and instead of "and this interpretation," it should read "and the first interpretation is not possible"—and all the above is resolved. And as for why the commentators of the Tosafos copied the first interpretation and not the second—it is *kal v'chomer* that they simply did not notice all the aforementioned difficulties.

***

292

Printed in Likutei Sichos, Vol. 17, p. 475.

**Month of Geulah:** It has not been clarified whether this refers to the month of Nissan—as in letter 283 above, or to the month of Kislev—as in letters 1953-3 above.

## 293

293

B"H, Erev Rosh Chodesh Iyar, 5713
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

...Regarding what you wrote about "From where will my help come?" (*Me'ayin yavo ezri*): It is well known how this is interpreted according to Chassidus—that this is not merely a question, but also the answer: **that from *Ayin* (Nothingness)**—which refers to *Atika Kadisha*—there is no "left side" (*d'leit smala*) in this *Atik*. Consequently, it draws down good without any concealments or hiddenness, and it is He who provides help in every situation, at every time, and in every place (see *Zohar HaKadosh*, *Parshas Beshalach* 64b, *Parshas Shelach* 158b), and this is explained in *Likutei Torah*, *Shemini Atzeres* 90b.

With blessings.

## 294

B"H, 5 Iyar, 5713
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

Your letter from this past Erev Shabbos has just been received. It is surprising that you do not confirm receipt of my letter containing holiday greetings for Pesach; perhaps it was delayed in the mail.

Regarding what you asked of me:

1) Concerning the cataract surgery that the doctors are urging you to undergo: since you write "the doctors" in the plural, there are surely at least two specialists among them. Consequently, there is no reason to hesitate or overthink this matter. Such surgeries have become a very routine procedure, and the necessary precautions are well known. From this, it is also understood that, on the face of it, traveling to another country for this purpose is not worthwhile, for several reasons. As for which doctor to choose, there is a story I heard from my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory: At the bris milah of one of the Tzemach Tzedek’s grandsons, which took place during the Tzemach Tzedek’s lifetime, they asked the Tzemach Tzedek which mohel to choose—the older one who knows the kavanos, or the younger one who is simply more experienced in the act itself. The Tzemach Tzedek replied that they should choose the younger one, because the main thing is the actual performance of the mitzvah. May Hashem send His word and heal you through the hand of this doctor, a complete and speedy recovery.

4) Regarding what you mentioned—that one of anash should sit and transcribe the *bichelech* (small booklets/manuscripts): for some time now, ever since I learned of the *bichelech*, I have requested of anash that if it is possible to clarify the content of the *bichelech*, they should occupy themselves with it and prepare indices. Perhaps this is what you had in mind.

With blessings for true health, awaiting good news.

## 255

B"H, 5 Iyar, 5713
Brooklyn.

To Anash in Melbourne, and in particular to the administration of Oholei Yosef Yitzchak there,
May Hashem grant them life.

Peace and blessing!

In response to your letter of 23 Nissan... What is immediately necessary is a critique of the manner in which the development and administration of the Yeshiva have been handled until now—a true critique, *aliba d'emes*. It is essential that you take into account that this is not your private institution; rather, it is the first Lubavitch institution founded in the country of Australia. And as one sets the foundation, so it continues. Therefore, my hope is strong that each and every one will completely set aside self-justification and personal bias, and will critique the matter according to the requirements of the institution’s welfare—which, in truth, is also the welfare of Anash in general, and of those involved in the administration of the Yeshiva in particular. This applies not only in spiritual matters but also in material ones, as explained in the writings: even after Yaakov had the promise that the Shechinah would descend with him to Mitzrayim, and he was therefore certain that he and his descendants would ascend from Mitzrayim—since they went into exile to Mitzrayim, the Shechinah was with them—nevertheless, this came to fruition through his sending Yehudah ahead of him, to prepare the way before him, and as our Sages of blessed memory say, to establish a house of study for him.

In accordance with the results of the aforementioned critique, may all of you strengthen yourselves in improving the situation. I am confident that, just as until now, my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, Nasi Yisrael, will bestow success and blessing, etc.

We spoke here at a farbrengen that even if the early ones were like angels, etc., if the early ones were like human beings, etc.—nevertheless, what our generation has merited is that the task of completing the work of *birurim* and the matter of *makeh b'patish* (the final blow of the hammer) has been placed upon us. It is a known ruling of our Sages that a mitzvah is named after the one who completes it. Consequently, it is easily understood—even by one who is not particularly insightful—the magnitude of the responsibility placed upon us from both sides: both from the aspect of completing the work of *birurim* and *makeh b'patish*, and on the other hand, that the entire work is called by our name. Therefore, I do not know if it is necessary to have *mesiras nefesh* specifically in order to discuss the situation *emesdige* (truthfully), especially since the merit of our forefathers and our Nasi, the holy one of Israel, my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, assists you.

I await good news, and I bless you that through success in the work of your hands, you will fulfill your mission, with peace of mind and material abundance.

## B'Tzav

B"H, 5 Iyar, 5713
Brooklyn.

Increasing the time for Chassidus study for the bochurim from Morocco is very appropriate, but it must be: 1) A proportional increase in quantity. 2) It must come through the *is'arusa d'lesata* (initiative from below) of the bochurim themselves. Through *kinas sofrim* (the jealousy of scribes that increases wisdom), this can surely be arranged.

## 298

B"H, 6 Iyar, 5713
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, and distinguished, etc., R' Yechiel,
May he be a shochet and bodek,

Shalom u’Bracha!

...Regarding what you wrote about the custom not to cut a boy’s hair until he reaches the age of three: You will find this at the end of the sefer *Shivim Temarim* (which concludes there that a child’s first haircut is a mitzvah, and see what is written there in *Be’er Heitev* and *Sha’arei Teshuvah* to *Orach Chaim* 531). This is also brought in the *Siddur* of the Maharid, for the son of the Tzemach Tzedek (where he also explains the matters according to the teachings of Chassidus). I have also heard this several times from my father-in-law, the Rebbe—and a section of his letter regarding the conduct at that time is transcribed below. Consequently, it is very appropriate that you also fulfill this. It is already known that the ruling is that "a Jewish custom is Torah." See also the Rambam at the beginning of *Hilchos Mamrim*, which implies that a custom accepted by all of Israel enters the category of a Torah-level positive or negative commandment. See *Choshen Mishpat* 25 and the *Biurim* in subsection 22; and *Hilchos Talmud Torah* of the Alter Rebbe, beginning of chapter 2.

In the sefer *Keser Shem Tov* (by Rabbi Gagin), page 591, it is written that in Eretz Yisrael they follow the aforementioned custom when the boy reaches four years of age! Perhaps this is a typographical error and it should say three years.

With blessings, and awaiting good news.

## 299

B"H, 8 Iyar, 5713
Brooklyn

Greetings and blessings!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letters from the 2nd and 5th of Iyar, along with the enclosures.

2) Regarding what you wrote about this year’s deficit being over four thousand dollars: in general, there is no reason to be discouraged. It might be worthwhile to conduct a special campaign to clear the debts, which would not interfere with the annual campaign and regular income; at the very least, any interference would be negligible, and even then, it would be worth it. I have long written to several of our institutions that the matter of income and expenditure for an institution of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, is not as they think—that because there is high expenditure, one must reduce the number of students, etc. Rather, the solution is to increase the channels of income. Since everything that is drawn down in the physical realm is first drawn down in the spiritual realm, and the channels of income in the spiritual realm are the students and the holy studies they engage in, one must first and foremost increase the *hashpaah* at the source by increasing the quantity and quality of the study and the quantity and quality of the students. Only afterwards should one endeavor to draw it down into the physical realm below. The practical outcome is clear and simple.

3) Regarding what you wrote about the time the *ramim* spend on business: this does not address the argument. The argument was not that they walk the streets during the time they are supposed to be learning in the *yeshiva*, but rather that their minds are wandering in the marketplaces and streets. Proof of this is that their clothing, etc., necessary for business is already prepared, and immediately upon the conclusion of the studies according to the schedule, the books are closed in the blink of an eye. They stand ready with their attire, and in that same instant, they transform from those who sit in the *beis medrash* to those who sit on street corners, running to the place where they imagine their livelihood and the livelihood of their families—may they live—is found. Consequently, it is impossible that even during the time of study, they are not preparing their thoughts and manner of speech for the business to which they turn immediately upon finishing the *shiur*.

4) The excessive time spent on secular studies and physical exercise compared to the time spent on holy studies is alarming. And "shall he even force the queen with me in the house?!"

May Hashem help each one of us not to exchange the essential of essentials for the trivial of the trivial, for such an exchange is inherently absurd, and furthermore, it even ruins the trivial matters, as is understood.

With blessings for success in your holy work.

P.S. Enclosed is the *kuntres* published for the upcoming Lag B'Omer, which I am sure you will use to bring merit to the many.

In continuation of the content of my letter, this *maamar* also explains the great importance of focus, that it is specifically through this that the matter comes into actualization; see there. The story is well-known, told by my father-in-law, the Rebbe, in the name of the Chassid R' Binyamin Kletzker, that in one of his business ledgers, he wrote the total as "There is none else besides Him." When asked how it is possible to occupy oneself with Chassidic matters while engaged in business, he replied that it is a *kal v'chomer*: if, at a time when one stands before the Holy One, blessed be He, one can fall into the "Kletzker's business calculations," then certainly, when one is occupied with "Kletzker's business," there is room for pure and holy thoughts to enter.

It would be worthwhile to explain this story to all of *anash* who are businessmen, and all the more so to those of *anash* who, according to the will of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, were supposed to be "dwellers of the tent," but have recently done something new—turning into businessmen. Any further elaboration on all the above is superfluous.

## 202

B"H, 8 Iyar, 5713
Brooklyn

To the esteemed Rabbi and Chossid, G-d-fearing and learned, occupied with communal needs, etc., our teacher Rabbi Gershon, may he live, and his wife, the Rabbanit Gessia, may she live,

Peace and blessing!

I was pleased to receive your letter from last week and to read that you, too, are seeing with your own eyes great improvement in your situation. May Hashem grant that each and every one of you will continue to share only good news with one another, speedily and in our days.

2) For some time now, I have been drawing the attention of all those who teach classes to the public (and even to individuals) to the fact that among the lessons, there should also be a lesson on the halachos that are common and regular in daily life, most of which are found in Shulchan Aruch Orach Chayim. If, due to a lack of time, it is impossible to do otherwise, they should be taught from the Kitzur Shulchan Aruch or from the halachos printed in various Siddurim, such as Derech HaChayim, Chavat Da’as, and the like. The main thing is that they should know the practical actions they must perform, literally—that is, regarding the blessings on food, interruptions during davening, etc., etc.

The greatness of the merit in this—that one establishes a person upon his purpose, which is "the whole of man: fear Hashem and keep His mitzvos"—is explained in many places and requires no further explanation. This is especially true when the one teaching the class is a Rav, who is naturally given special strengths to succeed in his influence upon his surroundings.

3) In response to your description of your communal involvement, particularly in the field of education: the immense value of this work is immeasurable. Consequently, in my opinion, there is no room at all for thoughts of abandoning it. However, the time has come to recognize the absolute necessity of abandoning the path of compromise. While in previous years there may have been room for debate as to whether it was worthwhile to concede a part of the essential in order to save the other part of the essential (and I write "room for debate" precisely, because the conclusion even then was that if one begins by conceding a part of the essential, eventually one concedes the whole of it), it is now clear, after the experience of decades.

We see that the path of various and strange compromises has led to terrible results. It is obvious that one must stand firmly on the foundation of Torah education without any compromises. Only then can one hope to save the younger generation, and consequently, the middle-aged and the elderly as well. Furthermore, the experience of the last generation has shown that the youth themselves mock those who follow the path of compromise, saying that if these compromisers truly believed in the ideas they were teaching, there would be no room for compromise—especially in matters connected to the Torah of Hashem, where it is understood that flesh and blood have no authority to make compromises in the matters of the Ribono shel Olam.

With blessings for true health and for good news, speedily and in our days.

P.S. Enclosed is the Kuntres published for the upcoming Lag B’Omer, with which I am sure you will merit the public.

## 201

B"H, 8 Iyar, 5713
Brooklyn

Greetings and blessings!

I acknowledge receipt of your letter from 6 Iyar. I am pleased that you have finally addressed the question of whether there might be aspects of your conduct that have caused a lack of rapport between yourself and your congregants. It is understood that there are two reasons for this, corresponding to the two categories of a person’s conduct: his conduct regarding himself, and his relationship with the people in his environment.

Regarding the second category: I believe I have already told you that you should take more interest in the personal affairs of your congregants, even if they do not pertain to matters of rabbinics, Torah, and mitzvos. When a person sees that the principle of "love your fellow as yourself" (even if it is not precise that it be exactly "as yourself") is being fulfilled toward him, then the verse "as water reflects a face to a face" is fulfilled in him as well.

Regarding the first category: It is clear that what you write is not the cause of the distance—namely, the fact that you conduct yourself according to the Shulchan Aruch, such as being careful about eating in others' homes, or regarding haircuts during Chol Hamoed and the days of Sefirah, and the like. On the contrary, the reason is precisely the opposite. The people of this country, even without paying attention to their own conduct, want to see in the other person—especially in one they have chosen as their leader—that there are no compromises. In general, any matter of compromise is suspected of being rooted in concessions and a lack of belief in the very matter for which the compromise is being made.

As for you, if there is any room for concern that your conduct in yiras shamayim is causing distance, it is not because you do not shave during Chol Hamoed or the days of Sefirah; rather, it is because you do shave (even with a chemical) during the rest of the year, and likewise in other matters. If the congregants are particular, then even if they do not say it explicitly, your standing in their eyes is diminished, and they are repelled when they see you engaging in compromises—and a word to the wise is sufficient. May Hashem grant you success in reaching a good arrangement, both materially and spiritually, for yourself and for your entire family, may they live.

Enclosed is the Kuntres published for the upcoming Lag B'Omer. In it, you will see an explanation of the concept of "paying attention," etc., and how it is precisely through this that things are brought into reality—and this applies to matters of Torah study and the fulfillment of mitzvos, etc., as is understood.

With blessings.

## 2102

B"H, 12 Iyar, 5713
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letters from the 25th and 28th of Nissan. You have surely received my letter in the meantime with holiday greetings, although you do not mention it in your aforementioned letters.

2) Regarding what you wrote concerning the rescue of children from the mission: My intention was not that they should rescue one or two children in one place and do the same in another, and then place them with private individuals. While the magnitude of such an act is immeasurable—and one who sustains a single Jewish soul is as if he has sustained an entire world—nonetheless, my proposal was for organized work and the establishment of an educational institution for children, which should consist of at least 50 boys or girls, with a suitable director, teachers, and so on. It is understood that there is no necessity to begin immediately with forty children; it is possible to start with ten, but in any case, it should not be done in the manner you describe. Beyond the matter of rescuing the children themselves, this would also provide a livelihood for a director, teachers, and so on. Just as in the human body, the health of one limb strengthens the other limbs, so too could we hope that through this institution, there would be an increase in strength for the other institutions as well—especially since Mr. Cole [shlita] promised me that if there were a suitable institution, they would provide the majority of its budget from the various offices...

I was pleased by what you wrote about meeting Mr. Shazar when he was purchasing a copy of the letters of our Alter Rebbe that was recently published, and about your conversation with him. There is a well-known parable regarding this: when there is a coal with a small spark, and one blows air upon it, one strengthens the spark and causes it to grow. It is the same when speaking with a person whose spark of soul, for various reasons, is not burning with a blazing fire; by blowing a spirit of life upon it, one assists him in strengthening the spark and increasing it, and no one knows the potential results and the benefit that may come from this...

The *Tehillim* *Yehe'el Ohr* was just received.

With blessings.

Enclosed is the *kuntres* published for Lag B'Omer, with which I am sure you will merit the public.

**Rescue of children from the mission:** See above, letter 1885.

## 203

B"H, 12 Iyar, 5713
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

...And here is an appropriate place for a general observation: While it is true that from all anash, and especially from the communal activists among them, we demand a firm stance—a *shtarken shtel*—to defend Torah and mitzvos in general, and Chassidus in particular, wherever they may be, one must always, at all times and in all places, distinguish and differentiate between the matters against which one must stand up, and the people with whom one must conduct oneself with ways of pleasantness and ways of peace. That is to say, even in a situation where it is necessary to express an open opinion against a certain matter, one must be as careful as humanly possible to avoid harming anyone’s dignity. Aside from the well-known teaching of our Sages that "sins should cease, but not sinners," this approach is actually beneficial to the struggle and brings closer the victory of the cause being defended and fought for. Since the other side also clearly sees that the intention here is not to diminish the stature of individuals—*nar men meint dem emes* (but rather, one intends only the truth)—and surely, further elaboration on this is superfluous, as the matter is sufficiently clear.

With blessings for success in your holy work, and may that success be in both material and spiritual matters alike.

Enclosed is the *kuntres* published for the upcoming Lag B’Omer, which I am certain you will merit to use to inspire the many.

## 204

B"H, 12 Iyar, 5713
Brooklyn

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter, in which you write regarding the question of where you will settle after your wedding—may it be in a good and auspicious hour—and you write your reasons for leaning toward settling in Eretz Yisrael, may it be rebuilt and established, in proximity to your parents, may they live and be well. You conclude by saying that if one is worthy, Hashem sends His blessing wherever a person lives, and if not, then G-d forbid, etc.

While it is within Hashem’s power to send His blessing without regard for the place where a person settles, it is well known from the statements of our Sages in many places that, nevertheless, a person should arrange their place of residence in a location and manner that is more conducive to settling down according to reason and the ways of nature, without needing to receive their livelihood in a supernatural or miraculous way. Even though the entire matter of livelihood generally depends on one’s *mazal*, it is nonetheless a known instruction of our holy Torah that a person must make an effort in all their affairs through natural means, and pray to the Master of all that He grant success within nature in a way that is above nature.

In accordance with the above, regarding your specific question as well, one should lean more toward settling in a place where your livelihood is more accessible—both material livelihood and spiritual livelihood. Even though, in general, a person does not know how they will earn their living, it is understood that in a place and country that offers greater and more varied possibilities, and where there is room to utilize the knowledge and talents that a person possesses, the possibility of settling down is easier. For these reasons, my opinion leans more toward considering settling in the United States...

It is understood that this is not a final, absolute decision that cannot be reconsidered, and it is possible that in time you will see various possibilities that are not apparent at the moment. But as things stand now, my opinion is as stated above.

Your sentiment, that you long to settle in proximity to your parents, may they live and be well, is certainly understandable and obvious. Nevertheless, one should not forgo something essential—which concerns many years and even decades—because of feelings of longing, etc. Even though this is connected to an inner chord of the soul, it is the way of the world that sons and daughters settle on their own. If the possibilities are more favorable in a place far from the parents, this has already become the way of the world. Furthermore, to a certain extent, this helps sons and daughters settle independently, and consequently reveals their inner strengths, since, by being at a distance, they know that it is necessary for them to manage in such a manner.

Regarding what you write about working in the field of education, etc., with the proper effort, such work—or something similar—exists in every place and country.

With blessings for a happy settlement, both materially and spiritually, and for the building of an eternal edifice upon the foundations of Torah and mitzvos, as they are illuminated by the teachings of Chassidus.

B"H, 13 Iyar, 5713

The Administration of Yeshivas Tomchei Temimim in Lod

Likewise, for those who are training in the craft of *shechitah*, on the face of it, this is no reason for them to avoid demonstrating their aptitude and knowledge in *Nigleh* and *Chassidus*. Surely, regarding the others as well, a way must be found so that this does not involve any matter of diminishing their standing, *chas v'shalom*, since the knowledge is only for my own purposes. Furthermore, the examiners will certainly not publicize the results at all, which will, in any case, make it possible for those who have avoided this until now to be tested...

With blessings for success in your holy work.

## B'Kav

B'Kav

B"H, 13 Iyar, 5713
Brooklyn

To the Administration of Tzeirei Agudas Chabad in Eretz Yisrael, may it be rebuilt,
May they live long,

Shalom u'Bracha!

Your letter to me was surely received in due time, and it is somewhat surprising that its receipt was not acknowledged, etc.

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter dated 23 Nissan—albeit with some delay—and today your telegram was received, to which I replied as follows: "Printing the *reshima* of the *likut* for *Mem-Dalet* and the introduction to *Kuntres Ayin-Ches* is advisable. With blessings, my signature." It is understood that from the introduction, one must omit that which pertains to the *Kuntres*.

I enjoyed reading what you wrote, albeit briefly, regarding your activities during the month of Nissan and the festival of Pesach. Surely, on the upcoming Lag B'Omer, you will conduct this with the appropriate publicity, similar to last year; however, in every matter of holiness, the ruling of our Sages is well known: *ma'alin bakodesh*—one must always increase in holiness.

Enclosed is a check as my participation in the expenses of the publication.

Regarding your question as to what is the primary focus of the intent of the Agudas Chabad and Tzeirei Agudas Chabad organization: I am surprised by this question, for it is known that Chassidim must occupy themselves with the dissemination of the teachings of Chassidus, its ways, and its customs, and this is their primary task. However, due to the unique situation of our current time, and especially in a specific location, it is self-understood that you must also strive to strengthen Judaism in general, and establish Torah classes in public—in the revealed Torah, and in places where there are no people, etc. As for defining the boundaries of where to work in the first category and where in the second, it is understood that this must be decided on the spot. Our *Nesi'im* have guided us in this as well, especially in the last generation, showing the path of working with *mesiras nefesh* to strengthen Judaism and Torah in general. Yet, it is understood and obvious that their work as the *Nesi'im* of Chabad was in the dissemination of the teachings of Chassidus, its customs, and its guidance, etc. With the proper approach, the work in the first category actually assists the work in the second.

Regarding what you wrote, that it is difficult to impose work upon the *avreichim* who are family men: it is a simple matter that one must impose work upon them, for the words of our Sages apply here as well: "More than the householder does for the poor man, the poor man does for the householder." Consequently, this is for the benefit of the *avreichim* even more than for those who receive from them. However, the nature of the work, its quantity, and its substance must be considered to a certain degree in accordance with the personal situation of each individual.

Regarding your statement that there are few workers and participants in your work in places other than Tel Aviv and Sefaria: one of the ways to draw new members more deeply into the work is to make them *mashpi'im* in their own cities and surroundings. This solves two problems: the lack of people on the spot, and how to bring those who have recently joined closer. It is understood that for responsible work, the person chosen must also be responsible. But *lo alman Yisrael*—the Jewish people are not widowed of leadership—and without a doubt, in every single place, one will find a *yarei shamayim* who possesses a sense of responsibility—a responsibility that, at the very least, if they do not know what to do, they will ask and investigate. As is known regarding the conduct of the Gaon, the Rogatchover, in granting *semicha*: he would be meticulous that the person be a *yarei shamayim*, saying that it is difficult to find someone whose knowledge of Torah will satisfy him completely. But if he were certain of the *yiras shamayim* of the one receiving *semicha*, that is a guarantee that if he does not know, he will not issue a ruling until he investigates, inquires, and finds the ruling.

Regarding your question as to whether it is worthwhile to reprint a *sicha* or *maamar* from those that have already been printed: seemingly, since your distribution is limited, there is no purpose in publishing once again what has already been printed without them. Especially since when one sends printed material via the post, it is a method whose effectiveness is very, very weak in drawing more people into the work.

Regarding the study of Tanya via a radio class: it is correct, provided that you are certain that the explanation and the emphasis are appropriate to the subjects. And if, as you say, it will be in *Lashon HaKodesh*, it is easier to be careful in this regard...

Thank you for sending the newspaper clippings here in which my letter for the festival of Pesach was printed. Surely you will continue to do so in the future, and I thank you in advance.

With blessings for success in your holy work, awaiting good tidings.

## 207

B"H, 13 Iyar, 5713
Brooklyn.

To the Rav, the Gaon, the Chassid, G-d-fearing, and distinguished, who engages in communal needs, etc.,
Rabbi R. Sh. Y. (may he live)

Greetings and blessings!

...Regarding what you wrote concerning matters of education:

1) It was certainly not my intention that all education be handed over to religious individuals, but only those schools belonging to the religious streams.

2) Likewise, it was not my intention that education be handed over to unknown individuals; on the contrary, it should be handled specifically by experts in education. Furthermore, it could even be the same people who have been involved until now, provided that they separate themselves from the party, so that education will have no connection whatsoever to political matters. In this way, the difficulties that you mention in your letter are removed.

3) Regarding what you heard from Mr. Gelman (may he live)—that the subject of our conversation was the difference between Chabad schools and Mizrachi schools regarding the aforementioned matter—this is very strange to me, as we did not sit upon this bench at all. My argument was—as per the content and wording of the copy of my letter that was sent to you—that it is understood this has no connection to schools that follow a **non-partisan** stream (such as Chabad).

4) Enclosed is the Kuntres published for the upcoming Lag B'Omer.

***

**Rabbi R. Sh. Y.:** Zevin. Additional letters to him—see above, Vol. 6, 1565, and the references in the notes there.

**Gelman... our conversation:** See above, Vol. 5, letter 1359.

## B'Kz

B"H, 15 Iyar, 5713
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 7 Iyar, in which you write that as a teacher in Bais Yaakov, you wish to know if the rumor is true that the study of Gemara has been permitted for girls, though you do not specify which Gemara or in what manner.

The necessary methods of study depend on the character of the students, their talents, and their knowledge. The foundations for determining the details of this are according to what is explained in the laws of Talmud Torah, in Yoreh Deah, section 246, paragraph 3, and the commentaries there. The essence and final conclusion can be found in the Hilchos Talmud Torah of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe, author of the Shulchan Aruch, at the end of chapter 1, which states: "Women are also obligated to learn the laws that they need to know, such as the laws of Niddah, Tevilah, salting [meat], the prohibition of Yichud, and the like. They are warned regarding **all** positive commandments that are not time-bound, and **all** negative commandments of the Torah and of the Sages, just like men."

In addition to this, in our days, when the foundations of religion have weakened in the minds of the masses—and especially the youth—and external cunning has already entered into them, it is essential that the study, particularly for young women—upon whom the entire conduct of the home will depend in a few years—be directed toward informing them of the beauty of Torah and familiarizing them with the perspective of our holy Torah on the very issues they encounter in their daily lives as a wife to her husband and as a mother to her children. The experience of recent years has proven just how necessary this knowledge is.

In accordance with this, in recent years it is impossible otherwise for young women to not have, at the very least, knowledge of the fundamental principles of the teachings of Chassidus, which brings about a deeper inner feeling regarding matters between man and Hashem—love of Hashem and fear of Him, Ahavas Yisrael, and love of Torah, etc. And if this knowledge is necessary for individual men and women, then how much more so is its necessity for those engaged in education, who have a special influence on the younger generation. Consequently, even a slight error or a minor deviation from the straight path by a teacher or educator is liable to cause great damage to all those they educate, and further elaboration on this is superfluous.

It is surprising that you do not mention more details in your letter regarding your work as a teacher, as well as regarding the students in the environment where you are located.

With blessings for success in elevating your students on the path that ascends to the House of Hashem.

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

The Secretary

## 208

B"H, 15 Iyar, 5713
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings,

In response to your letter, in which you ask regarding the fact that, based on what you found written in certain books, you had a silver ring made with holy names engraved upon it, and you are asking for my opinion as to whether you should wear it on your hand as a *segulah*, etc.

Regarding this, based on what you know, in your environment and in the place where you are located, only women wear rings. Consequently, there is a concern regarding the prohibition of "a man shall not wear..." [Devarim 22:5]. This is in addition to the matter of the sanctity of the names engraved on the ring, which requires great caution—both regarding the places one passes through or enters, and regarding the sanctity of the names themselves, given that many books have become corrupted, especially those books printed without any *haskamah* at all, or without *haskamos* from known, reliable individuals. Therefore, for all these reasons, in my opinion, you should not wear the ring. At most, since you have already taken the action and the names have been engraved, you should keep the ring in the room where you sleep.

Regarding a *segulah* for *chen* (favor), you should increase your involvement in the letters of Torah and *tefillah*. Furthermore, you should have engraved in the "brain of your memory" certain chapters of *Tehillim*, certain chapters of *Mishnayos*, and certain chapters of *Tanya*. Then, according to what is explained in *Likutei Torah* at the end of *Parshas Kedoshim*, even at a time when you are not actively thinking about them, they are nonetheless engraved in the "brain of your memory." May Hashem grant you success in the study of the *nigleh* of Torah and the *Toras HaChassidus* with *yiras shamayim*, at which point there will be no place for the aforementioned matter.

With blessings for success in Torah and *yiras shamayim*.

## 208*

B"H, 30th of Tammuz, 5713
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the distinguished philanthropist, a man of stature, G-d-fearing,
Energetic activist,
A man of many accomplishments, adorned with noble and lofty character traits,
Etc., Mr. Yitzchak
Almaleh, may he live and be well,

Peace and blessing!

Through the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing and refined, engaged in communal needs, etc., etc., Rabbi Shlomo Matosov, may he live, I learned of the note he received from the youths. It appears that these youths fit the description of our Sages of blessed memory as being "shaken" (nena'arim) from Torah and mitzvos. It is surely unnecessary to point out that one should pay no attention to this whatsoever, other than to this point: the very fact that it angered them so much is proof that your efforts in rescuing the children—the sons and daughters of Yisrael—from the net of heresy, Heaven forfend, and bringing them closer to fear of Heaven and to Torah and mitzvos, have borne fruit, thank G-d. It is understood that one must increase one's courage in this and stand firmly at one's post; for if one who sustains a single soul of Yisrael is considered as if he sustained an entire world, how much more so is this true for one who sustains many souls of Yisrael.

This matter is sweetened even further by the saying of the Baal Shem Tov, may his merit protect us, which we heard from the mouth of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, that the preciousness of every single man and woman of Yisrael before the Holy One, Blessed be He, is incomparably greater than the preciousness of an only child to parents born to them in their old age. Further elaboration for one such as yourself is unnecessary.

May the Name, blessed and exalted, lengthen your days and years—good and healthy years—in strengthening Yiddishkeit in the spirit of our ancestors, with pure fear of Heaven.

With a blessing for success and for receiving the Torah with joy and inner feeling.
